O, children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the final part of medical science, for its perfection and completion at the end, and for its explanations and clarifications, it seems to me that I must present to you this treatise, which is a branch of manual operation, that is surgery.
Because manual operation is neglected in our region and time, to the point that it greatly endangers medical science, and its practice is hindered; and nothing remains of it except a few brief descriptions in the books of the ancients, which are misunderstood by most people and lead them to error and hesitation while their minds are closed and their strength, skill, and art are extended.
It is evident to me that I must revive it through the arrangement of this treatise according to a method of exposition, clarification, and abbreviation; also by adding drawings of cautery tools and other instruments required for the operation, as well as their explanations, which are necessary for its preparation.
The reason why no good surgeon is found nowadays with a hand in our region is that medical art requires long training and its practitioner must previously be skilled in the science of anatomy which includes knowledge of the use of body parts, their forms, the way they are joined or separated from each other; the location of bones, nerves, and ligaments as well as their number and exit points; those veins that pulse and those that remain still, and also their locations.
And for this reason, Hippocrates says that it has a great name but a small effect, and especially in the treatment of the hand.
We have already mentioned this in the introduction of this book; for those who are unaware of what we have discussed regarding anatomy, they may not be convinced and could fall into error, which has led to the deaths of many men, as I myself have witnessed such cases where some were learned in theory but lacked practical knowledge.
I once saw an inexperienced doctor accidentally cut into a woman's neck, severing some arteries and causing severe blood loss. Unfortunately, the woman died due to excessive bleeding.
I witnessed another case where a doctor was about to remove a large stone from a patient's bladder using part of the bladder itself. However, due to this procedure, the patient died three days later.
I was called upon to extract such a stone myself, and due to the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I knew that death would be imminent.
I observed another surgeon who received compensation from a duke in our land. This surgeon operated on an impoverished Black man with a fractured hip; however, due to the surgeon's ignorance, he removed the fracture directly over the wound, using powder and strong ligatures without allowing any air to reach the wound.

Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse regarding the consideration of apostemes and their incision.
These apostemes are specific spots, of many shapes, according to their recollection, as described in this book.
And they are diverse in their piercing and incision due to two methods: one is specific to the nature of the apostemes themselves, and another for those that arise from moistures.
The second method is specific to the locations where they occur in the body, as an accidental aposteme on the head differs from that on the buttocks; and an aposteme in a fleshy area differs from one in a joint; and each of them has its own treatment for operation.
And there are some apostemes that should not be pierced, but rather left to mature or fill with healthy matter.

Chapter Two: On fractures of the skull (or head).
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, with distinct causes. Some fractures occur due to the impact of space (or force), driving all the bones into the skull, similar to how it happens in wood; this type is called "ascia." Or, a fracture may occur when an object penetrates part of the bone but does not pierce through, only affecting its outer layer and flesh without reaching the brain. This latter kind is known as "levatio absoluta.
The severity of these two fracture types can vary greatly, and there is another kind of fracture known as "contrusio" or "contusi," caused by blunt force trauma from an object like a club or a fall onto rocks, which resembles the former two in its appearance.
This fracture can be either penetrating, nearly reaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it can affect only the surface of the bone, and in both cases, their severity can range from minor to severe.

Chapter 23.
Regarding a discussion on dislocations.
Dislocation is the evident displacement of one of the body's joints from its original position, prohibiting movement, and disfiguring the affected limb, causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, one must immediately attempt to perforate and reduce it, without delay in reducing the joint, for if it is delayed, the joint will become inflamed and very stiff due to this, making reduction of the dislocation difficult.

When it is necessary to treat the dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder joint is often dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns to its place with difficulty; it can be dislocated in all directions, but mainly forward or backward. The dislocation is not hidden from you when examining under the arm or through touch, regardless of how it's positioned. When you adjust the dislocated shoulder with your hand, it becomes clearly evident because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm or reach its own muscle.
And be aware that when treating it, you must reduce the dislocation either forward or backward before any abscess forms, because if an abscess develops, reducing the dislocation becomes challenging and the patient may not fully recover; this is especially true for backward dislocations as they are the worst of all types, causing severe pain and often leading to death.
The restoration process for the shoulder joint, when possible to reduce it, involves extending the patient's hand with both hands and spreading their arm outward; then place your own hands on top of the shoulder and underneath it, pushing the joint back into place either by using finger pressure or the base of your palm.
If the dislocation is in the front part of the body, then you should return it by bending the arm at the elbow and striking the elbow against the base of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not respond to reduction attempts, then administer vigorous extension with great force.
Have two ministers extend the arm, with each one holding a part of it to ensure that it is not pulled away due to the extension.
Then the arm should be rotated in all directions while being wrapped with a folded cloth or bandage around its hand.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should apply some oil to it so that these measures promote lubrication of the joint and facilitate its movement. Then, push on the joint with vigorous force while reducing it back into place.
The reduction process requires that the patient be placed over a plaster cast made of a mixture of egg white and alum, with strict immobility measures applied to their arm, which should be suspended from the neck using a sling for several days.
And then it should be untied; if the joint is in its place, you can untie the bandage and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not strong enough, then you should reapply the plaster and bandage, leaving it for a few days until it heals; and then you can untie it.
However, if the patient experiences a relapse of joint pain and their condition worsens with movement, administer moist hot applications and gentle friction, along with compression until it improves; or place an oily poultice over the joint - in case of excessive grease; then bandage it tightly, leaving the binding on during both day and night. Then remove the binding and place it in a warm bath; when it cools down, you should rub the joint with warm friction, and then apply an oily poultice again if needed - in case of excess moisture or soreness - three times: once, twice, and thrice. With the aid of a bath instrument, until it improves.
And if you wish, place over it a moist and scraped cow's bladder with lard on top of that, doing so several times as the moisture is reduced.

For the treatment of spinal dislocation.
When there is a complete dislocation of one of the vertebrae in the back or neck, and many vertebrae are displaced from their original positions, then it is incurable because death approaches the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excretion happens involuntarily and they cannot control it; their limbs become limp or both legs, or one leg, or an arm may be affected. Or there is dislocation of a vertebra from its place, which often leads to either displacement or removal in four different directions.
Thus, it has been removed to the rear parts and is called 'gelbozitat'.
However, the treatment of this condition is to be considered if it occurs in childhood, as then there may be a chance for cure and full recovery.
But for those cases of dislocation that occur due to heavy falls or blows, as well as those similar to them, the Ancients have described methods of treatment in great detail, and I refer you to their works for further information; but I will summarize briefly what is sufficient here without going into too much detail. My fear of exceeding my intended length has led me to write concisely, using various instruments which I shall demonstrate.
And there is only one cure for this, which I recommend, in the case of inflammation that specifically affects the back.

Then below each wheel there should be a minister who turns the wheel with their hand, and the doctor treats the spinal cord as we have mentioned before.

On healing a dislocated hip."
Sure! Here is the translation:
The hip joint can be dislocated in four ways: inwardly, outwardly, forward, or backward.
It is mostly dislocated inwardly, and rarely forward or backward.
The sign of inward dislocation is as follows: when you align the patient's affected leg with their healthy one, it will be longer and the heel will be more prominent than that of the healthy side. The patient cannot bend their foot inwards, and the area where one would normally find a hernia becomes swollen and develops an abscess due to trapped pus, indicating that the head of the femur has reached this point.
The sign of outward dislocation is the opposite of what was previously described for inward dislocation.
The sign of forward dislocation is when the patient stretches their leg fully but cannot bend it at the knee due to pain in the heel area. If they attempt to walk, they cannot step forward; instead, they must rely on their ankle joint for ambulation.

De curacio de dislocacio del denolh.
The eyeball is dislocated in three ways: it is dislocated to the sides outwards, and to the sides inwards, and further back towards behind; but not forward toward the front.
The sign of dislocation is that you instruct the patient to place their leg on their thigh, and if it doesn't move or resist, then you know the eye is dislocated.
To restore it in all three ways, make the patient stand up straight and extend their leg if they can; press your two hands against their face, place their foot between your feet, then stick the eyeball to your palms, hold those together firmly over the eye with strength, while another assistant extends the patient's leg until the eye returns to its socket.
The sign of successful restoration is when their leg sticks to the thigh without exertion or resistance.
Then plaster it there and make the leg stick to the thigh, bind those together with a bandage for three or four days, then untie them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for a few days until they are healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction of it to you is not required, then provide strong extension with linen bandages that preceded the narration for the cure of the hip.
Chapter 33.
On the cure of dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes slightly twisted due to minor displacement, and at other times it is dislocated according to the injury, with the dislocation occurring either inside or outside.
The sign of the ankle's dislocation is that you see swelling proceeding from the injury towards which it is dislocated.
But the cure for its minor displacement is gentle reduction, and it should be extended with ease using the hands and wrapped with a bandage while returning to its place.
In the cure for a severe dislocation, it is necessary to make the patient lie down straight, and let there be one strong and valiant assistant behind them at their back.
And then you have your right hand on the patient's foot at the uppermost part, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot towards the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and use the left twice, then extend it to the left; finally move the heel of the foot with your right hand below the leg without touching it.
Do this twice, as we have counted.
And then move the heel of the foot to the leg for the third time, you are at the ankle.
So if you return in one or two movements according to this manner, and see the foot flexed, it is good; but if not, then repeat the operation until it does.
However, if you are unable to reduce it by what we have recommended, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and strap a thick stick firmly into the ground, that is placed between their thighs, and ensure they are wrapped in cloth so they do not get cold.
E aprop le ministre de lu tengua las suas coyssas, e aprop autre ministre extenda le pe am las suas mas o am liament que lie sobre le col del pe; e aprop extenda aquel quascun ministre contre extencio del sieu par, e le payssel sia extant entre las coyssas del malaute retinent aquel, per so que·l cos de lu no yesqua a las partidas dejos deves la sua extencio.
And then the doctor grasps the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant holds the healthy limb at the affected parts, as the dislocation easily returns to its place.
So, when the dislocation returns and you ensure the health of the reduced limb, then apply a plaster cast and bind it tightly with bandages, securing the foot to the leg.
Be cautious and avoid the nerves that run behind the ankle, as you don't want the binding to be too tight and injure those nerves severely.
Then leave it like that for two or three days; if the binding becomes loose, remove it.
And then untie the binding on the third or fourth day, but forbid the patient from walking for forty days. However, if the patient is forced to walk before that time, it should be noted that the dislocation might not have fully healed and could potentially be re-aggravated, leading to a situation where it becomes incurable.
If an acute abscess occurs in the patient, you should apply to it the same treatment as described earlier (in this treatise), along with other appropriate locations for cure and embrocation until it is healed.
Chapter .34.
Regarding the treatment of dislocation in the toes of the feet.
Ensure that the one who is dislocated has limited movement and this should not be difficult but rather gentle.
However, if the dislocation is in any of the bones at the back of the foot, then you should make the patient rest their foot on a spot on the ground or a table, and keep it there as if they were standing.
Then stand on foot, and place your foot over the dislocated area, then press it with force until it returns to its place, and ensure that it is now aligned and does not stick out at all.
Then place under the sole of their foot a table that covers it entirely, this should have two ends and you must strap it with appropriate tension and firmness for three days; then untie them and make them rest from walking for many days, until they are strong again and recovery is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the different types of dislocations that can be caused by trauma or fracture or any other mishap.
When any of these accidents happen, and you proceed with the treatment and restoration of the patient, many unfortunate events can lead to death.
And it is not appropriate to be presumptuous about the treatment of the patient, but rather one should be wise in their practice, experienced, merciful, compassionate, calm, and not hasty or boastful; they should use initial treatments that cause mild abscesses only, and leave the ultimate healing to God's judgment, unless you are certain that salvation from destruction is expected, with relief from suffering and some hope for recovery. Therefore, strive to make the reduction of the dislocation as soon as possible at the beginning of treatment, before it develops into a severe abscess.
And if the limb returns to its proper position as desired, then apply a regimen that allows for mild abscess formation and treat the wound with appropriate drying ointments.
If the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and there are separated bone fragments, then you must align those pieces, doing as we have previously stated for simple injuries of this kind.
